		<item>
		<title>Teaching models to forget: Selective unlearning with Amazon Nova</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/teaching-models-to-forget-selective-unlearning-with-amazon-nova/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Qian Hu]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 06 Jul 2026 22:23:45 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Announcements]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Foundational (100)]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">e451c5cb5932ddbe103cc2baf499c9ded6565a70</guid>

					<description>In this post, we introduce Reverse Direct Preference Optimization (rDPO), the novel unlearning technique behind Amazon Nova Customizable Content Moderation Settings (CCMS), and show how it reduces over-deflection while preserving model quality. We also provide pointers for customers who want to apply these preference optimization techniques to their own experiments.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Organizations deploying foundation models (FMs) often encounter a common challenge: model safeguards designed for content moderation can also prevent legitimate, business-critical use cases. A media company summarizing scripts with mature language, a cyber security firm simulating real-world threats, or a legal team processing sensitive evidence may all find that default content moderation controls deflect the very content they need to work with. For example, a security team asking the model to generate a sample phishing email for employee awareness training may receive a refusal, even though the intent is defensive.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Because the model learns these safeguards during post-training alignment, prompt engineering alone cannot overcome them. The model’s tendency to deflect is embedded in its parameters, requiring a targeted modification at the model level to selectively adjust this behavior. In this post, we introduce &lt;em&gt;Reverse Direct Preference Optimization (rDPO)&lt;/em&gt;, the novel unlearning technique behind Amazon Nova Customizable Content Moderation Settings (CCMS), and show how it reduces over-deflection while preserving model quality. We also provide pointers for customers who want to apply these preference optimization techniques to their own experiments.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/nova/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova&lt;/a&gt; &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/nova/latest/userguide/customizable-content-moderation.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Customizable Content Moderation Settings (CCMS)&lt;/a&gt; addresses this by letting approved customers selectively adjust safeguards across four responsible AI (RAI) pillars. These pillars encompass:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Safety – Covering dangerous activities, weapons, and controlled substances.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Sensitive content – Including profanity, nudity, and bullying.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Fairness – Considerations around bias and culture.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Security – Concerns involving malware and malicious content.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Nova enforces essential, non-configurable controls for responsible use of AI, such as controls to prevent harm to children and preserve privacy.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The science behind CCMS is &lt;em&gt;unlearning&lt;/em&gt;, a technique for selectively removing learned behaviors from a model’s parameters without retraining from scratch. We train Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) adapters to reverse the model’s alignment to specific policies. The result is a custom model variant that generates content in customer-approved policy areas while remaining aligned everywhere else.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution"&gt;Solution&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="lora-adapter-training"&gt;LoRA adapter training&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;CCMS works by providing customers with a LoRA adapter that has been trained to unlearn specific RAI policies from the core model. When a customer imports this adapter, they receive a custom model identified by a unique Amazon Resource Name (ARN). At inference time, the adapter steers the core model away from deflecting content in the customer’s approved policy areas. Additionally, Nova’s output moderation guardrails are configured for the customer’s approved policies when using that custom model ARN.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="rdpo-a-novel-approach-for-unlearning"&gt;rDPO: A novel approach for unlearning&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The key scientific challenge is performing this unlearning effectively: the model must stop deflecting content in targeted policy areas while preserving its general capabilities (instruction following, coding, math) and maintaining alignment in non-targeted areas. A direct fine-tuning approach risks degrading overall model quality.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;One state-of-the-art approach is &lt;a href="https://arxiv.org/abs/2404.05868" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Negative Preference Optimization (NPO)&lt;/a&gt;, which builds upon Direct Preference Optimization (DPO). DPO is a preference optimization approach that trains the model to rank the preferred response higher than a dispreferred one, relative to a reference model. It moves the model toward the preferred response and away from the dispreferred one. NPO achieves unlearning by removing positive samples from the optimization objective, effectively training the model to move away from its learned deflection behavior. However, NPO only teaches the model to forget without guiding it toward high-quality alternative responses, which can result in degraded output quality.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To address this, we developed rDPO, which reverses the preference pair in the DPO objective. Rather than simply making the model forget, rDPO simultaneously guides it toward generating high-quality responses in the unlearned policy areas. This dual objective not only produces better response quality but also improves training efficiency, requiring fewer optimization steps to converge.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following shows the comparison of DPO, NPO, and rDPO loss functions:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/ML-21116-1.png" alt="Side-by-side loss function equations for DPO, NPO, and rDPO" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In DPO loss, &lt;span class="math inline"&gt;&lt;em&gt;y&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;em&gt;w&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/span&gt;, &lt;span class="math inline"&gt;&lt;em&gt;y&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;em&gt;l&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/span&gt; are the preferred and dispreferred response, respectively, where &lt;span class="math inline"&gt;&lt;em&gt;w&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/span&gt; and &lt;span class="math inline"&gt;&lt;em&gt;l&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/span&gt; mean win and lose responses, respectively. The loss function optimizes the model to rank preferred response higher than the dispreferred one. In NPO loss, &lt;span class="math inline"&gt;&lt;em&gt;y&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;em&gt;f&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/span&gt; is the response to forget, the NPO loss trains the model to move away from &lt;span class="math inline"&gt;&lt;em&gt;y&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;em&gt;f&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/span&gt;. In rDPO, &lt;span class="math inline"&gt;&lt;em&gt;y&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;em&gt;t&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/span&gt; is the target response and &lt;span class="math inline"&gt;&lt;em&gt;y&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;em&gt;f&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/span&gt; is the forgetting response. The rDPO loss function teaches the model to move away from the forgetting response while moving close to the target response. We call our approach rDPO as the training objectives are the reverse of the DPO applied in the original model’s post-training stage.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following figures show the training dynamics of NPO and rDPO and their performance on the evaluation dataset.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/ML-21116-2.png" alt="Training accuracy over optimization steps for NPO and rDPO, with rDPO converging near 1 by step 30" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/ML-21116-3.png" alt="Training reward over optimization steps for NPO and rDPO, with rDPO increasing while NPO decreases" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;From the training dynamics, we can see that the training accuracy of rDPO converges to almost 1 at around step 30, and the training accuracy of NPO does not change too much. The training rewards of the chosen response (aka target response) of rDPO continues growing while that of the NPO continues dropping. This implies that rDPO moves the behavior of the model to the target response, while it’s hard for NPO to move the model away from the forgetting response and to the target response. This is likely because the base model has strong RAI alignment, making it difficult to move away from safe responses.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We can see similar trends on NPO and rDPO’s performance on the evaluation dataset. The training dynamics plots show that rDPO is much more efficient than NPO in terms of unlearning performance.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="customization-pipeline"&gt;Customization pipeline&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following figure shows the model customization pipeline.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-full wp-image-134932" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/Image-7-6-26-at-9.44 AM.png" alt="" width="774" height="175"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;It starts with data preparation, where a set of prompts is curated to represent the target policy areas where we want to unlearn alignment behaviors. Given those prompts, we generate the forgetting and target responses. We train a model with rDPO and the generated data. Once the training loop finishes, the LoRA adapter is exported and ready for serving. We choose LoRA fine-tuning because of its training efficiency and lower inference cost compared to full-rank fine-tuning.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="results"&gt;Results&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We evaluate the RAI customized model on two dimensions: (1) reduction in over-deflection on sensitive requests, and (2) preservation of general model capabilities.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Deflection rate measures the percentage of prompts that the model refuses to answer. For the baseline model, a high deflection rate on sensitive content indicates that legitimate requests are being blocked. A lower deflection rate after customization means the model is now able to process those requests successfully. The following table shows deflection rates across five evaluation categories:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Model&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Red Team Prompts&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Fairness&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Safety&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Security&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sensitive Content&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Baseline&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;98.10%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;51.84%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;86.51%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;91.61%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;79.02%&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;RAI customized model&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;47%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;23.83%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;32.77%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;45.73%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;33.58%&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The results show substantial reductions in deflection rate across all policy categories. Safety deflections dropped from 86.51 percent to 32.77 percent (a 53.74 percentage point reduction). The customized model now processes the majority of safety-related requests that the baseline would have refused. Similar improvements are seen across Fairness (28 pp reduction), Security (46 pp), and Sensitive Content (45 pp). Importantly, these reductions are achieved through the rDPO-trained LoRA adapter without any modification to the base model weights.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A critical requirement for any unlearning approach is that it must not degrade the model’s general capabilities. To verify this, we evaluate the customized model on three utility benchmarks: instruction following, mathematical reasoning (Math Mini), and code generation (MBXP Python):&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Model&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Instruction Following&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Math Mini&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;MBXP Python&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Baseline&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;94.12%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;86.40%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;74.80%&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;RAI customized model&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;92.57%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;85.20%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;73%&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The customized model retains near-baseline performance across all utility benchmarks, with drops of only 1.55 percentage points on instruction following, 1.20 pp on math, and 1.80 pp on code generation. These minimal degradations confirm that the rDPO approach successfully targets only the RAI alignment parameters while leaving the model’s core capabilities intact, a key advantage of the LoRA-based approach over full model fine-tuning.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="getting-started"&gt;Getting started&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The preference optimization techniques described in this post, including DPO, NPO, and rDPO, are accessible to customers who want to explore unlearning and alignment research on their own. Amazon SageMaker AI supports DPO training with both full-rank and LoRA approaches for Amazon Nova and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/model-customization/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;over 20 open-weight models&lt;/a&gt;, and Amazon SageMaker HyperPod provides ready-made DPO recipes that can serve as a starting point for custom unlearning experiments. See &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/customize-amazon-nova-in-amazon-sagemaker-ai-using-direct-preference-optimization/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Customize Amazon Nova in Amazon SageMaker AI using Direct Preference Optimization&lt;/a&gt; for a step-by-step walkthrough.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For customers who want the benefits of unlearning without building it themselves, CCMS provides pre-trained LoRA adapters for Amazon Nova ready to deploy. Adapters are shared through &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ram/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Resource Access Manager&lt;/a&gt; (AWS RAM). After you &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/ram/latest/userguide/working-with-shared-invitations.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;accept&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/copy-model-copy.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;copy&lt;/a&gt; an adapter into your account, it appears as a custom model in Amazon Bedrock with a unique ARN. You then &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/deploy-custom-model-on-demand.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;create an on-demand inference custom model deployment&lt;/a&gt; and use the custom model deployment ARN in &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/APIReference/API_runtime_Converse.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;the standard Converse API&lt;/a&gt;. No other code changes are required:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws bedrock-runtime converse \
  --model-id "arn:aws:bedrock:us-east-1:&amp;lt;account-id&amp;gt;:custom-model-deployment/&amp;lt;deployment-id&amp;gt;" \
  --region us-east-1 \
  --messages '[{"role": "user", "content": [{"text": "Your prompt here"}]}]'&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; Deploying a custom model incurs standard Amazon Bedrock inference charges. See Amazon Bedrock pricing for details.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The customized model behaves identically to the base Nova model for non-configured policy areas. For additional application-level safeguards, you can pair CCMS with &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/guardrails/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock Guardrails&lt;/a&gt; to implement topic-specific filtering, hallucination detection, or custom content policies on top of the configured model.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To request access and discuss which policy pillars to configure for your use case, reach out to your AWS account team or create a support case from the &lt;a href="https://support.console.aws.amazon.com/support/home#/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Support Center Console&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we introduced Reverse DPO (rDPO), a preference-based unlearning technique. It addresses a key limitation of existing approaches like NPO: the inability to simultaneously guide the model toward high-quality responses while unlearning targeted behaviors. By reversing the preference pair in the DPO objective, rDPO achieves both goals in a single training signal, converging faster and producing better output quality than NPO. Requiring fewer training iterations, rDPO also reduces the time and cost of producing customized adapters for customers.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Our evaluation on &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/nova/models/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova 2 Lite&lt;/a&gt; demonstrates that rDPO-trained LoRA adapters reduce deflection rates by up to 54 percentage points across RAI policy categories while degrading utility benchmarks by less than 2 percentage points. Importantly, these customizations operate within the bounds of Amazon Nova universal, &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/nova/latest/userguide/responsible-use.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;non-configurable protections&lt;/a&gt;, ensuring that core safeguards remain intact regardless of adapter configuration. The LoRA-based approach is particularly well-suited to this problem: it isolates unlearning to a small set of adapter parameters, preserves the base model’s integrity, and enables modular deployment where different policy configurations can be served from the same foundation model.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;These techniques power &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/nova/latest/userguide/customizable-content-moderation.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova&lt;/a&gt; CCMS, which provides customers with pre-trained adapters ready to deploy using Amazon Bedrock. For researchers and practitioners interested in further exploring preference-based unlearning, Amazon SageMaker AI offers &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws/sagemaker-hyperpod-recipes?tab=readme-ov-file#fine-tuning" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;DPO training recipes&lt;/a&gt; that can serve as a foundation for custom experimentation. For further instructions on customizing Nova in Amazon SageMaker AI, see our &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/nova/latest/nova2-userguide/nova-model.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;documentation&lt;/a&gt; and blogs on &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/aws/announcing-amazon-nova-customization-in-amazon-sagemaker-ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Nova customization&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/advanced-fine-tuning-methods-on-amazon-sagemaker-ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;advanced fine-tuning methods&lt;/a&gt; on Amazon SageMaker AI.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="acknowledgement"&gt;Acknowledgement&lt;/h2&gt;

		<title>Run MiniMax models on Amazon Bedrock</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/run-minimax-models-on-amazon-bedrock/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Zohreh Norouzi]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 06 Jul 2026 17:00:44 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Intermediate (200)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">3080a7799ade422bbe55136044702a7b0a6a88bb</guid>

					<description>In this post, we walk through how to get started with MiniMax models on Amazon Bedrock, including the capabilities supported by these models, the service tiers available, how on-demand inference scales to handle your workloads, and the different APIs you can use to access them. Using these models, customers can build agentic applications, long-context document analysis pipelines, and software engineering workflows, all backed by the security and operational guarantees of AWS.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Organizations are increasingly adopting open-weight foundation models (FMs) to power production AI workloads, from agentic coding assistants to long-context document analysis. As these workloads move from experimentation to enterprise deployment, two requirements shape every model selection decision: the model must deliver the capabilities the workload demands, and the inference environment must support the organization’s security and compliance requirements. Customers are looking for ways to access frontier third-party models without compromising on data protection, regulatory alignment, or operational control.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To address this need, Amazon Bedrock offers a fully managed service for accessing leading FMs from independent model providers, with inference running entirely on AWS-operated infrastructure. Your prompts and completions are not used to train any models, and your content is not shared with the model providers.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href="https://minimax.io" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MiniMax&lt;/a&gt; family is available on Amazon Bedrock, giving you three open-weight models to match different production workloads. The family is purpose-built for software engineering and agentic use cases. The newest model on Amazon Bedrock, &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/model-card-minimax-minimax-m2-5.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MiniMax M2.5&lt;/a&gt;, is trained specifically for agent-native execution. The following sections cover what each model offers and how to choose between them.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we walk through how to get started with MiniMax models on Amazon Bedrock, including the capabilities supported by these models, the service tiers available, how on-demand inference scales to handle your workloads, and the different APIs you can use to access them. Using these models, customers can build agentic applications, long-context document analysis pipelines, and software engineering workflows, all backed by the security and operational guarantees of AWS.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="about-minimax"&gt;About MiniMax&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://minimax.io/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MiniMax&lt;/a&gt; is a global AI technology company that develops multimodal foundation models with a research emphasis on efficient architectures for production-scale workloads. Its M2 family of large language models is available on Amazon Bedrock as fully managed open-weight models, built around a mixture-of-experts (MoE) architecture where only a small fraction of total parameters activate per token, delivering the knowledge capacity of a much larger dense model at a fraction of the inference cost.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The M2 family delivers strong performance on coding and agentic workloads, with M2.5 purpose-built for agent-native execution through training that emphasizes tool-calling, multi-step task decomposition, and long-horizon coding tasks. Because the models are open-weight, you can independently evaluate the model architecture and training methodology, run your own benchmarks on representative workloads, and fine-tune on proprietary data when customization is required. You can do all of this through a fully managed AWS service, without provisioning infrastructure, hosting model weights, or operating inference stacks.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For the full model catalog, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/model-cards-minimax.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MiniMax models on Amazon Bedrock documentation&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="minimax-models-on-amazon-bedrock"&gt;MiniMax models on Amazon Bedrock&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock supports three models from the MiniMax M2 family. MiniMax M2 (&lt;code&gt;minimax.minimax-m2&lt;/code&gt;) was the first to launch, establishing the core capabilities of the series with strong multilingual text generation, solid reasoning and coding performance, and a 1 million token context window. MiniMax M2.1 (&lt;code&gt;minimax.minimax-m2.1&lt;/code&gt;) followed, adding targeted improvements to reasoning depth, coding accuracy, and instruction following. MiniMax M2.5 (&lt;code&gt;minimax.minimax-m2.5&lt;/code&gt;) is the newest model available on Amazon Bedrock and is trained specifically for agent-native execution. Amazon Bedrock continues to expand its catalog of MiniMax models as new versions become available. For the latest list, refer to the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/model-cards-minimax.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock model documentation for MiniMax&lt;/a&gt;. The following table summarizes the key differences.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;MiniMax M2&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;MiniMax M2.1&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;MiniMax M2.5&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Model ID&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;minimax.minimax-m2&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;minimax.minimax-m2.1&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;minimax.minimax-m2.5&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Context window&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;1M tokens&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;196K tokens&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;196K tokens&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Max output tokens&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;8K&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;8K&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;8K&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Training focus&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Multilingual, reasoning, coding&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Improved reasoning, coding, instruction following&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Agent-native, reinforcement learning (RL) on agentic scaffolds&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Service tiers&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Standard, Priority, Flex&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Standard, Priority, Flex&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Standard, Priority, Flex&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Best for&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Long-context or multilingual, general-purpose&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Complex instruction-following or multi-step reasoning&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Agentic, tool-calling, or coding-heavy&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;MiniMax M2.5 uses a &lt;a href="https://huggingface.co/MiniMaxAI/MiniMax-M2" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;mixture-of-experts&lt;/a&gt; (MoE) architecture with 230 billion total parameters and 10 billion active per token. For inference cost, the MoE routing mechanism is the key factor. It provides the knowledge capacity of a 230B model while consuming compute proportional to only 10B active parameters per forward pass.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="two-endpoints-for-accessing-minimax-models-on-amazon-bedrock"&gt;Two endpoints for accessing MiniMax models on Amazon Bedrock&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock provides two endpoints for invoking MiniMax models: &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;bedrock-runtime&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Amazon &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; endpoint (&lt;code&gt;https://bedrock-mantle.{region}.api.aws/v1&lt;/code&gt;) is the public API for Amazon Bedrock’s next-generation inference engine. It uses the Chat Completions API, the same interface as the OpenAI Python and TypeScript SDKs, so teams already on that SDK can switch to MiniMax models on Amazon Bedrock by updating the base URL and model ID. It supports Amazon Bedrock API keys, projects, and client-side tool calling. For most workloads, we recommend the &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; endpoint.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;code&gt;bedrock-runtime&lt;/code&gt; endpoint (&lt;code&gt;https://bedrock-runtime.{region}.amazonaws.com&lt;/code&gt;) uses the Converse and InvokeModel APIs via the AWS SDK. Use this endpoint for native Amazon Bedrock features such as Guardrails, Agents, Flows, and model evaluation, which are currently available through &lt;code&gt;bedrock-runtime&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In the following sections, we demonstrate both endpoints, starting with the recommended &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; endpoint and the Chat Completions API.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="getting-started-with-minimax-m2.5-in-amazon-bedrock"&gt;Getting started with MiniMax M2.5 in Amazon Bedrock&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Complete the following steps to start using MiniMax M2.5 in Amazon Bedrock.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="console-playground"&gt;Console playground&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Navigate to the &lt;a href="https://console.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock console&lt;/a&gt; and select &lt;strong&gt;Chat/Text playground&lt;/strong&gt; from the left menu under the &lt;strong&gt;Test&lt;/strong&gt; section.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Select model&lt;/strong&gt; in the center of the playground.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;MiniMax&lt;/strong&gt; from the category list, then select &lt;strong&gt;MiniMax M2.5&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Apply&lt;/strong&gt; to load the model.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Confirm that the model loaded. The model name appears in the playground header and the chat interface is ready for input.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To demonstrate M2.5’s reasoning and code generation capabilities, try the following prompt in the playground:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;“Design a Python microservice that exposes a REST API for managing a task queue. Include error handling, input validation, and write unit tests. Explain your design decisions.”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="using-the-bedrock-mantle-endpoint-recommended"&gt;Using the bedrock-mantle endpoint (recommended)&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For the &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; endpoint, you need an &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/api-keys.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock API key&lt;/a&gt; or AWS credentials configured for SigV4. To provide access to the &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; endpoint, use the following minimum policy:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
    "Version": "2012-10-17",
    "Statement": [
        {
            "Sid": "BedrockMantleInference",
            "Effect": "Allow",
            "Action": [
                "bedrock-mantle:CreateInference",
                "bedrock-mantle:Get*",
                "bedrock-mantle:List*"
            ],
            "Resource": "arn:aws:bedrock-mantle:us-east-1:111122223333:project/*"
        },
        {
            "Sid": "BedrockMantleApiKeyAccess",
            "Effect": "Allow",
            "Action": "bedrock-mantle:CallWithBearerToken",
            "Resource": "*"
        }
    ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Replace 111122223333 with your AWS account ID, and scope the AWS Region to the Regions that you use. The first statement covers SigV4 authentication. The second covers Amazon Bedrock API key (bearer token) authentication. If you only use SigV4, you can omit the second statement. To control which identities can generate or use Amazon Bedrock API keys, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/api-keys-permissions.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Control permissions for generating and using Amazon Bedrock API keys&lt;/a&gt;. To restrict your organization to approved models only, use a &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/organizations/latest/userguide/orgs_manage_policies_scps.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;service control policy (SCP)&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following example uses the &lt;a href="https://github.com/openai/openai-python" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;OpenAI Python SDK&lt;/a&gt; as a client library to call the &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; endpoint. When using the OpenAI SDK, you need an &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/api-keys.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock API key.&lt;/a&gt; For production workloads, use short-term API keys, which expire automatically (maximum 12 hours) and inherit the permissions of the AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) role that generated them. If you’re already using AWS credentials and don’t have an API key, the &lt;a href="https://pypi.org/project/aws-bedrock-token-generator/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;aws-bedrock-token-generator&lt;/a&gt; package generates a short-term bearer token from those credentials.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; The code examples in this post invoke MiniMax M2.5, and each model invocation incurs per-token charges. See the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock pricing page&lt;/a&gt; for current rates.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import boto3
from openai import OpenAI

# Retrieve the Amazon Bedrock API key from AWS Secrets Manager
secrets_client = boto3.client("secretsmanager", region_name="us-east-1")
api_key = secrets_client.get_secret_value(SecretId="bedrock-api-key")["SecretString"]

client = OpenAI(
    base_url="https://bedrock-mantle.us-east-1.api.aws/v1",
    api_key=api_key,
)

response = client.chat.completions.create(
    model="minimax.minimax-m2.5",
    messages=[
        {"role": "user", "content": "Explain the benefits of mixture-of-experts architectures for production inference."}
    ],
    max_tokens=512,
)

print(response.choices[0].message.content)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; These examples retrieve the Amazon Bedrock API key from &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/secretsmanager/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Secrets Manager&lt;/a&gt;. For local development, you can instead read the key from an environment variable, but avoid that pattern in production. Use AWS Secrets Manager or another secrets store.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="tool-calling"&gt;Tool calling&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;MiniMax M2.5 is designed for agentic workflows, making it well-suited for tool-calling scenarios. In a tool-calling workflow, you define functions (tools) that the model can invoke, the model decides when to call them based on the user’s request, and your application runs the function and returns the result for the model to incorporate into its final response.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following example demonstrates this pattern end to end. We define a &lt;code&gt;get_weather&lt;/code&gt; tool, send a user message, let the model request the tool call, run the function with mock data, and pass the result back so the model can generate a natural-language answer.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import json
import boto3
from openai import OpenAI

# Retrieve the Amazon Bedrock API key from AWS Secrets Manager
secrets_client = boto3.client("secretsmanager", region_name="us-east-1")
api_key = secrets_client.get_secret_value(SecretId="bedrock-api-key")["SecretString"]

client = OpenAI(
    base_url="https://bedrock-mantle.us-east-1.api.aws/v1",
    api_key=api_key,
)

tools = [
    {
        "type": "function",
        "function": {
            "name": "get_weather",
            "description": "Get the current weather for a given location",
            "parameters": {
                "type": "object",
                "properties": {
                    "location": {
                        "type": "string",
                        "description": "City and country (e.g., Seattle, US)"
                    },
                    "unit": {
                        "type": "string",
                        "enum": ["celsius", "fahrenheit"],
                        "description": "Temperature unit"
                    }
                },
                "required": ["location"]
            }
        }
    }
]

# Step 1: Send the user request with tool definitions
messages = [
    {"role": "user", "content": "What's the weather like in Seattle?"}
]

response = client.chat.completions.create(
    model="minimax.minimax-m2.5",
    messages=messages,
    tools=tools,
    tool_choice="auto",
)

assistant_message = response.choices[0].message

# Step 2: Check if the model wants to call a tool
if assistant_message.tool_calls:
    messages.append(assistant_message)

    for tool_call in assistant_message.tool_calls:
        function_name = tool_call.function.name
        arguments = json.loads(tool_call.function.arguments)

        # Step 3: Validate function name and run
        if function_name == "get_weather":
            location = arguments.get("location", "Unknown")
            unit = arguments.get("unit", "fahrenheit")
            result = {
                "location": location,
                "temperature": 18 if unit == "celsius" else 64,
                "unit": unit,
                "condition": "Partly cloudy",
                "humidity": 72,
            }
        else:
            result = {"error": f"Unknown function: {function_name}"}

        # Step 4: Return the function result to the model
        messages.append({
            "role": "tool",
            "tool_call_id": tool_call.id,
            "content": json.dumps(result),
        })

    # Step 5: Get the final response incorporating tool results
    final_response = client.chat.completions.create(
        model="minimax.minimax-m2.5",
        messages=messages,
        tools=tools,
    )

    print(final_response.choices[0].message.content)
else:
    print(assistant_message.content)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="using-the-bedrock-runtime-endpoint-boto3"&gt;Using the bedrock-runtime endpoint (boto3)&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="prerequisites-1"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For the &lt;code&gt;bedrock-runtime&lt;/code&gt; endpoint, you need AWS credentials configured (IAM user or role) with permission to invoke the model. Use the following minimum policy:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
    "Version": "2012-10-17",
    "Statement": [
        {
            "Effect": "Allow",
            "Action": [
                "bedrock:InvokeModel",
                "bedrock:InvokeModelWithResponseStream"
            ],
            "Resource": "arn:aws:bedrock:us-east-1::foundation-model/minimax.minimax-m2.5"
        }
    ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For production deployments, scope the Resource to the specific Regions you use. To restrict your organization to approved models only, use a &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/organizations/latest/userguide/orgs_manage_policies_scps.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;service control policy (SCP)&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following example sends a single-turn request to MiniMax M2.5 using the &lt;a href="https://boto3.amazonaws.com/v1/documentation/api/latest/index.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS SDK for Python (Boto3)&lt;/a&gt; with the Converse API and prints the model’s response:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import boto3

client = boto3.client("bedrock-runtime", region_name="us-east-1")

response = client.converse(
    modelId="minimax.minimax-m2.5",
    messages=[{
        "role": "user",
        "content": [{"text": "What is mixture of experts?"}]
    }],
    inferenceConfig={"maxTokens": 2048, "temperature": 1.0, "topP": 0.95},
)

content_blocks = response["output"]["message"]["content"]
response_text = next(
    (block["text"] for block in content_blocks if "text" in block),
    None
)

if response_text:
    print(response_text)
else:
    print("No text response.")&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; On the Converse API, MiniMax M2.5 returns a &lt;code&gt;reasoningContent&lt;/code&gt; block before the &lt;code&gt;text&lt;/code&gt; block. The code iterates through the content blocks to extract the final text response.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="using-the-aws-cli"&gt;Using the AWS CLI&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can also access MiniMax M2.5 from your terminal using the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cli/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Command Line Interface (AWS CLI)&lt;/a&gt;:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws bedrock-runtime converse \
  --model-id minimax.minimax-m2.5 \
  --messages '[{"role":"user","content":[{"text":"Type_Your_Prompt_Here"}]}]' \
  --inference-config '{"maxTokens":2048}' \
  --region us-east-1&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="service-tiers"&gt;Service tiers&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock offers multiple &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/service-tiers-inference.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;service tiers&lt;/a&gt; to match different workload requirements:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tier&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Best for&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Characteristics&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;MiniMax M2.5 support&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Priority&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Mission-critical, customer-facing workflows that need the fastest response times&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Up to 25% better output tokens per second (OTPS) latency compared to Standard. Prioritized ahead of Standard and Flex requests. Premium over standard on-demand pricing. No upfront reservation or commitment.&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Yes&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Standard&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Everyday AI tasks such as content generation, text analysis, and routine document processing&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Consistent performance at standard on-demand pricing. Default tier when no tier is specified. No commitment.&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Yes&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Flex&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Workloads that can tolerate longer processing times, such as model evaluations, content summarization, and agentic workflows&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Discounted pricing relative to Standard. Higher latency, especially during peak traffic, since Flex requests are processed after Standard. No commitment.&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Yes&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; The Reserved tier isn’t currently available for MiniMax models. For updates on Reserved tier availability, contact your AWS account team.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="scaling-on-demand-inference"&gt;Scaling on-demand inference&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When you invoke MiniMax models on Amazon Bedrock, requests use on-demand inference (Standard tier) by default, where you pay per token without reserving capacity. On-demand throughput is shared and allocated per AWS Region, so during periods of high regional demand a request may be briefly queued or throttled. Designing for this is important for applications that need to scale reliably in production.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;On the &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; endpoint, there is no requests-per-minute (RPM) quota. Throughput is governed by token-based limits rather than request counts. MiniMax models don’t currently have per-account token quotas published in the Service Quotas console, so their throughput is managed by the endpoint’s internal scheduling and capacity. Use retry logic with exponential backoff to handle transient throttling. Cached input tokens read through prompt caching don’t count against the input-token quota. For details, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/quotas-mantle.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quotas for the bedrock-mantle endpoint&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Error&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;What it means&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;What to do&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;HTTP 429&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;A token-per-minute quota for the model has been exceeded.&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Reduce the submission rate and retry with exponential backoff. Request a quota increase through AWS Support if you consistently hit the limit.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;HTTP 503&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Regional capacity for the model is under pressure.&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Retry with exponential backoff for transient errors. Reduce the submission rate for sustained errors.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The two errors call for different responses. For a 429, reduce your submission rate or request a quota increase through AWS Support. For a 503, retry transient errors and ramp gradually, as described in the following section.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Handle one-off 503 responses.&lt;/strong&gt; Some on-demand inference requests may see occasional 503 responses when the model is in high demand, and the recommended way to handle them is exponential backoff with jitter and a bounded retry count. The AWS SDK and most popular HTTP clients support this through standard retry configuration. The following example uses Boto3:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import boto3
from botocore.config import Config

config = Config(retries={"total_max_attempts": 6, "mode": "standard"})
client = boto3.client("bedrock-runtime", config=config)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If 503 responses become sustained, retries alone won’t resolve the issue because the effective request rate is exceeding available capacity for the model. In that case, consider routing latency-sensitive traffic to the Priority tier, which receives preferential processing ahead of Standard and Flex requests during periods of high demand.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Handle steep traffic ramps.&lt;/strong&gt; When using Standard tier on-demand inference, your application’s incoming traffic should align with how the model’s regional capacity scales. Sudden, large jumps in request rate are more likely to trigger 503s than gradual increases that the system can accommodate. Whenever you increase the request rate against MiniMax models, scale up in measured increments rather than stepping straight to a new target volume. The recommended ramp procedure is:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Start at your target request rate.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;If you receive 503 responses, reduce the rate by 50 percent, and continue reducing until requests are succeeding consistently.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Hold at that steady state for 15 minutes.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Increase the rate by 50 percent and hold for another 15 minutes.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Repeat until you reach your target volume.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;As a worked example, if your target is 2,000 requests per minute and you encounter 503s, you would reduce to 1,000, then to 500 if errors persist. After 500 is steady for 15 minutes, you scale to 750, then 1,125, and so on. The 15-minute hold is the part most teams skip, and it’s the part that matters most. Without it, every step up is essentially a fresh load test.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Choose the Priority tier for latency-sensitive workloads.&lt;/strong&gt; Beyond reactive use during sustained 503s, the Priority tier can be a useful lever to reduce occurrences of 503 while continuing to use on-demand inference. Priority delivers up to 25 percent better output tokens per second compared to Standard, and there is no upfront reservation or commitment. Applications opt in by setting the &lt;code&gt;service_tier&lt;/code&gt; parameter to &lt;code&gt;priority&lt;/code&gt; on each invocation, and tiers can be mixed within the same application. Customer-facing prompts, real-time agents, and other user interactions where response time directly affects experience are good candidates for Priority. For background and batch-style work, Standard or Flex is typically the right choice and avoids paying the Priority premium on requests that wouldn’t benefit from it.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Additional best practices for production scale.&lt;/strong&gt; A handful of additional practices help keep inference workloads running smoothly at scale. Spreading large workloads across multiple minutes, rather than firing them in tight bursts, reduces pressure on Regional capacity. When migrating production traffic to a new MiniMax model version, you can use feature flags to ramp the percentage of traffic gradually instead of cutting over all at once. Asynchronous work, such as model evaluations, content summarization, and agentic backfills, can be routed to the Flex tier, which is designed for cost-effective processing of latency-tolerant workloads. For workloads without data residency requirements, distributing across multiple Regions improves resilience during regional demand spikes. For workloads expected to grow, planning headroom for two to three times the expected peak provides a buffer for traffic surges.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For complete guidance, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/scaling-throughput-best-practices.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Scaling and throughput best practices&lt;/a&gt; in the Amazon Bedrock User Guide.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="reducing-latency-with-implicit-prompt-caching"&gt;Reducing latency with implicit prompt caching&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;MiniMax models on Amazon Bedrock support implicit prompt caching. When consecutive requests share a common prompt prefix, this might result in a cache hit, allowing the model to reuse the cached internal state instead of recomputing it. Cache hits reduce inference latency on the matching tokens, with no changes to your code and no cache markers required.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Implicit prompt caching is available across all on-demand service tiers (Standard, Priority, and Flex), so applications can take advantage of it regardless of how their traffic is routed. Cache hits are not guaranteed on every request, but they are common in workloads with stable prefixes. Examples include multi-turn agents, retrieval-augmented generation pipelines, and long-context analysis workflows where system prompts, tool definitions, or source documents are reused across requests. To maximize cache hit rates, place static content (system prompts, tool definitions, reference documents) at the beginning of the prompt and dynamic content (user messages, variable context) at the end.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;On-demand inference incurs charges only when you invoke a model, so there is no infrastructure to tear down. To avoid unintended charges, consider the following:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;If you generated short-term Amazon Bedrock API keys for testing, they expire automatically within 12 hours. To revoke one sooner, delete the API key in the &lt;a href="https://console.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock console&lt;/a&gt;. Deleting a key immediately revokes access for any application using it, so confirm no active applications depend on it first.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;If you opted in to the Priority tier for testing, remove the &lt;code&gt;service_tier&lt;/code&gt; parameter from your requests to return to Standard pricing for traffic that isn’t latency-sensitive.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="pricing-and-availability"&gt;Pricing and availability&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;MiniMax M2.5 is available in 14 AWS Regions: US East (N. Virginia), US East (Ohio), US West (Oregon), Europe (Frankfurt), Europe (Stockholm), Europe (Milan), Europe (Ireland), Europe (London), Asia Pacific (Tokyo), Asia Pacific (Mumbai), Asia Pacific (Sydney), Asia Pacific (Jakarta), Asia Pacific (Melbourne), and South America (São Paulo). Requests are served in the Region you call. Cross-Region inference (Geo and Global) is not currently available for MiniMax models. For the latest list, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/models-regions.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;supported Regions page&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Pricing is per token and varies by model and service tier. For current rates, see &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock pricing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we walked through how to get started with MiniMax M2 family models on Amazon Bedrock. We explored the two inference endpoints, &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;bedrock-runtime&lt;/code&gt;, demonstrated tool calling with the Chat Completions API, covered service tier options for matching workload requirements, and discussed scaling strategies including implicit prompt caching for latency reduction.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get started:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Open the &lt;a href="https://console.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock console&lt;/a&gt; and try MiniMax M2.5 in the Chat/Text playground.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Run the &lt;code&gt;bedrock-mantle&lt;/code&gt; Python sample from this post against your own data.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Evaluate MiniMax M2, M2.1, and M2.5 on your workloads to choose the model that fits your cost and latency profile.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;For production deployment, review the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/scaling-throughput-best-practices.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Scaling and throughput best practices&lt;/a&gt; and consider the Priority tier for latency-sensitive traffic.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="resources"&gt;Resources&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For more information, refer to the following resources:&lt;/p&gt;
