		<item>
		<title>Deploying Multi-Turn RL Infrastructure for Amazon Nova on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/deploying-multi-turn-rl-infrastructure-for-amazon-nova-on-amazon-sagemaker-hyperpod/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Maria Masood]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 06 Jul 2026 16:58:13 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker AI]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Expert (400)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">c18f360adbe8520fd770f4e042ba4a2f58edc96d</guid>

					<description>In this post, you deploy a two-phase infrastructure for multi-turn RL using Amazon Nova Forge on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod. By the end, you have an event-driven pipeline that starts training when you upload data to Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3). The training job teaches the model to play Wordle, a placeholder for your own RL task.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;When you build enterprise agents that execute multi-step workflows, you face a fundamental training challenge. These agents query databases, call APIs, cross-reference results, and recover from mid-process failures. The quality of any single action depends on what happens several steps later.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Standard &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/what-is/reinforcement-learning-from-human-feedback/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF)&lt;/a&gt; optimizes single responses in isolation. This approach falls short for multi-step workflows where an agent that validates data before proceeding prevents a cascade of downstream errors. Multi-turn reinforcement learning (RL) addresses this gap by optimizing over entire interaction sequences. Your agents learn tool orchestration, error recovery, and multi-step reasoning through trial and error. Supervised fine-tuning (SFT), &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/what-is/retrieval-augmented-generation/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;retrieval-augmented generation (RAG)&lt;/a&gt;, and continued pre-training are complementary techniques, but they typically do not teach these sequential decision-making capabilities on their own.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI&lt;/a&gt; also offers multi-turn RL as a fully managed, serverless capability, bringing this technique to SageMaker training jobs with no infrastructure to manage. When you need full control over the training stack: your own agent environment, custom orchestration, or specific instance configurations. For these cases, the multi-turn RL infrastructure for Amazon Nova on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod gives you the compute, orchestration, and reward-routing layers to train agents on these complex workflows. Amazon Nova delivers frontier intelligence and industry-leading price performance, and Amazon Nova Forge extends this with multi-turn RL training capabilities.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you deploy a two-phase infrastructure for multi-turn RL using Amazon Nova Forge on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod. By the end, you have an event-driven pipeline that starts training when you upload data to &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/s3/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3)&lt;/a&gt;. The training job teaches the model to play Wordle, a placeholder for your own RL task.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The solution is an event-driven pipeline: you upload a dataset to Amazon S3, and the infrastructure provisions compute, routes rewards, and runs multi-turn RL training automatically. Three layers do the work. A SageMaker HyperPod cluster generates responses and applies GRPO (Group Relative Policy Optimization) weight updates. ECS on AWS Fargate runs your reward environment. The Nova Forge SDK routes messages between the model and that environment while tracking conversation state across turns. AWS Step Functions orchestrates the run, triggered by Amazon EventBridge when data lands in S3.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The architecture is split into two phases: a one-time AWS Cloud Development Kit (AWS CDK) deployment provisions the long-lived foundation (VPC, EKS/HyperPod, ECS, S3, IAM, and the pipeline), while each training run spins up its own ephemeral resources. This keeps GPU compute from sitting idle between runs and lets you iterate without redeploying.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following diagram shows how these components interact during a training run.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/25/ML-20450-1.png" alt="Architecture diagram of the multi-turn RL pipeline spanning a SageMaker HyperPod cluster, ECS on Fargate reward workers, and the Nova Forge SDK proxy" width="600" height="736"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Multi-turn RL on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod infrastructure architecture&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The pipeline orchestrates a multi-turn conversation loop across three compute surfaces:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Amazon SageMaker HyperPod (EKS)&lt;/strong&gt;:&lt;/a&gt; Training primary, worker pods, and vLLM generation replicas run on P5 instances. The model generates responses. Training pods perform GRPO weight updates using reward signals.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;ECS on Fargate&lt;/strong&gt;: Reward workers run your environment (for example, Wordle or a custom Bring Your Own Orchestrator (BYOO) environment). They receive model responses via SQS, score them against your rubric, and return reward signals.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/nova/forge/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova Forge&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/strong&gt; : The SDK’s proxy layer routes messages between the model and the reward environment, tracking conversation state across turns.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before you deploy, make sure you have the following:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Amazon Nova Forge subscription&lt;/strong&gt;: You need this subscription to access the Nova Forge SDK and model training APIs.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;SageMaker HyperPod instance quota&lt;/strong&gt;: Minimum of 10 × &lt;code&gt;ml.p5.48xlarge&lt;/code&gt; for &lt;code&gt;generation_replicas: 4&lt;/code&gt;. Request 12–14 for production workloads. &lt;code&gt;ml.p5en.48xlarge&lt;/code&gt; is also supported.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Bootstrapped CDK environment&lt;/strong&gt;: Run &lt;code&gt;cdk bootstrap&lt;/code&gt; before your first deploy.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Python 3.12+&lt;/strong&gt;: The CDK app and AWS Lambda runtime require this version.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AWS CDK v2&lt;/strong&gt;: Install with &lt;code&gt;npm install -g aws-cdk&lt;/code&gt;. This synthesizes and deploys the AWS CloudFormation stack.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AWS Command Line Interface (AWS CLI) v2&lt;/strong&gt;: Configured with credentials that have permissions to create VPCs, EKS clusters, SageMaker HyperPod clusters, IAM roles, and Step Functions.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Docker&lt;/strong&gt;: You use Docker to build the Lambda container images that package the Nova Forge SDK.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;Important: This infrastructure costs approximately $786–$1,180 per hour when running (10–12 &lt;code&gt;ml.p5.48xlarge&lt;/code&gt; instances). Review the Cost breakdown section and plan to destroy the stack when you are not actively training.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="deploy-the-infrastructure"&gt;Deploy the infrastructure&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="clone-and-install"&gt;Clone and install&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Start by cloning the sample repository and installing the Python dependencies for the AWS CDK app. Run these commands from your local machine or development environment:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;git clone https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-nova-multi-turn-rl-infra.git
cd nova-multi-turn-rl-infra
pip install -r requirements.txt&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="configure"&gt;Configure&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Set all parameters in &lt;code&gt;cdk.json&lt;/code&gt; under the &lt;code&gt;context&lt;/code&gt; key. Two parameters are &lt;strong&gt;required&lt;/strong&gt; before your first deployment:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Parameter&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Description&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;project_tag&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Unique prefix for all resource names (for example, &lt;code&gt;my-nova-rl&lt;/code&gt;“. Becomes part of your EKS cluster name, HyperPod cluster name, and S3 bucket tags.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;sdk_resource_prefix&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Prefix for SDK-created resources (for example, &lt;code&gt;nrl-myproject&lt;/code&gt;). The Nova Forge SDK uses this when naming its CloudFormation stacks, Lambda functions, and SQS queues.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Key infrastructure parameters:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table style="width: 100%" border="1" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Parameter&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Default&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Description&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;instance_type&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;ml.p5.48xlarge&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;HyperPod instance type&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;instance_count&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;10&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Number of instances in the Restricted Instance Group&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;nova_model&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;NOVA_LITE_2&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Model to train: &lt;code&gt;NOVA_MICRO&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;NOVA_LITE&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;NOVA_LITE_2&lt;/code&gt;, or &lt;code&gt;NOVA_PRO&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;vf_env_id&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;wordle&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Built-in reward environment for validation&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;use_custom_env&lt;/code&gt; / &lt;code&gt;custom_env_id&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;—&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Set &lt;code&gt;use_custom_env&lt;/code&gt; to “true” and specify the directory name under &lt;code&gt;custom-environments/&lt;/code&gt; for BYOO&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;reward_cpu&lt;/code&gt; / &lt;code&gt;reward_memory&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;2048 CPU, 4096 MiB&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Fargate task sizing&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;eks_kubernetes_version&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;1.32&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;EKS cluster version&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Training parameters. These flow through the pipeline event to the training job:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table style="width: 100%" border="1" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Parameter&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Default&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Description&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;training_method&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;—&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;RFT_MULTITURN_FULL&lt;/code&gt; or &lt;code&gt;RFT_MULTITURN_LORA&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;max_steps&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;10&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Number of training steps&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;generation_replicas&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;4&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;vLLM generation replicas&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;global_batch_size&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;64&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Samples per training step&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can override any parameter at deploy time:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;cdk deploy -c instance_count=1 -c max_steps=20&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="deploy"&gt;Deploy&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;cdk deploy --require-approval never&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="two-phase-deployment-model"&gt;Two-phase deployment model&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The infrastructure follows a two-phase deployment model that separates long-lived foundational resources from ephemeral per-run resources. This separation reduces costs by creating expensive compute resources only when you need them, speeds up iteration by avoiding full redeployment for each training run, and simplifies management by giving each layer an independent lifecycle.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Phase 1: &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/cdk/v2/guide/home.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS CDK&lt;/a&gt; deploy (one-time):&lt;/strong&gt; When you run &lt;code&gt;cdk deploy&lt;/code&gt;, you provision the foundational infrastructure:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/vpc/latest/userguide/what-is-amazon-vpc.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Virtual Private Cloud (Amazon VPC)&lt;/a&gt; with private subnets and VPC endpoints.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/eks/latest/userguide/what-is-eks.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service (Amazon EKS)&lt;/a&gt; cluster with a HyperPod Restricted Instance Group (RIG).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/ecs/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Elastic Container Service (Amazon ECS)&lt;/a&gt; cluster on AWS Fargate for reward workers.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/s3/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3)&lt;/a&gt; bucket for training data and checkpoints.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/iam/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) roles&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/step-functions/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;An AWS Step Functions pipeline&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/eventbridge/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon EventBridge rules&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The deployment takes approximately 30-40 minutes. The majority of time is spent on EKS cluster creation (~15 minutes), followed by SageMaker HyperPod Helm chart installation via AWS CodeBuild (~5 minutes), SageMaker HyperPod cluster provisioning (~15-25 minutes, depending on P5 capacity), and Lambda container image builds (~5 minutes).&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Phase 2: Runtime (per training run):&lt;/strong&gt; When you upload a &lt;code&gt;.jsonl&lt;/code&gt; file to the S3 bucket’s &lt;code&gt;training-data/&lt;/code&gt; prefix, EventBridge triggers a Step Functions pipeline that creates the runtime resources for that training run. The &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws/nova-forge-sdk" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Nova Forge SDK&lt;/a&gt; deploys its own &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cloudformation/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS CloudFormation&lt;/a&gt; stack containing &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/lambda/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Lambda&lt;/a&gt; functions (conversation proxy), &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sqs/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Simple Queue Service (Amazon SQS)&lt;/a&gt; FIFO queues (message routing between model and environment), an &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/dynamodb/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon DynamoDB&lt;/a&gt; table (conversation state tracking), and ECS Fargate tasks (reward workers). The SDK manages the lifecycle of these resources.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="trigger-a-training-run"&gt;Trigger a training run&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After you deploy the infrastructure, you start a training run with a single S3 upload. EventBridge watches the bucket’s &lt;code&gt;training-data/&lt;/code&gt; prefix and starts the Step Functions pipeline automatically.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A successful training run shows increasing reward scores in the Amazon CloudWatch metrics over successive steps. For the Wordle environment, expect the model to converge within 50-100 steps, with average reward improving from near-zero to 0.6-0.8 as the model learns to narrow guesses based on feedback.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prepare-your-training-data"&gt;Prepare your training data&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;code&gt;.jsonl&lt;/code&gt; file uses a metadata-based format: each line contains a prompt and the ground-truth answer that the reward environment uses for scoring:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{"id": "wordle_train_001", "metadata": {"prompt": "Guess the 5-letter word", "answer": "crane"}}
{"id": "wordle_train_002", "metadata": {"prompt": "Guess the 5-letter word", "answer": "slate"}}
{"id": "wordle_train_003", "metadata": {"prompt": "Guess the 5-letter word", "answer": "plumb"}}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;code&gt;id&lt;/code&gt; field must be unique across all records. The model sees &lt;code&gt;metadata.prompt&lt;/code&gt; at the start of each conversation, and the reward environment uses &lt;code&gt;metadata.answer&lt;/code&gt; to score the model’s responses across turns. Each training example becomes a multi-turn conversation: the model generates a guess, receives feedback from the reward environment, and iterates until it solves the puzzle or exhausts its turns.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="upload-and-trigger-automatically"&gt;Upload and trigger automatically&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;# Get the bucket name from CDK outputs
BUCKET=$(aws cloudformation describe-stacks --stack-name NovaMultiTurnRlStack \
  --query "Stacks[0].Outputs[?OutputKey=='TrainingBucketName'].OutputValue" --output text)

# Upload:pipeline starts automatically
aws s3 cp training-data.jsonl s3://$BUCKET/training-data/training-data.jsonl&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When a file lands in the &lt;code&gt;training-data/&lt;/code&gt; prefix, EventBridge detects the PutObject event and invokes the &lt;code&gt;S3TriggerFn&lt;/code&gt; Lambda function. This function extracts the file path, merges it with the training parameters from &lt;code&gt;cdk.json&lt;/code&gt;, and passes the resulting event data to Step Functions, which executes the pipeline through five stages. You can watch progress in the Step Functions console, where each step shows its input, output, duration, and retry count.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="trigger-manually-for-ad-hoc-runs"&gt;Trigger manually for ad-hoc runs&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To iterate on parameters without re-uploading data, use the setup script to bypass EventBridge and invoke the pipeline directly:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;# Override training parameters for a quick test run
./scripts/setup.sh \
  --data-path s3://$BUCKET/training-data/training-data.jsonl \
  --max-steps 5 \
  --global-batch-size 32 \
  --training-method RFT_MULTITURN_LORA&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This helps during development: point at an existing dataset and experiment with different step counts, batch sizes, or the training method (full fine-tuning versus LoRA) without modifying &lt;code&gt;cdk.json&lt;/code&gt; or redeploying.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="monitor-and-debug"&gt;Monitor and debug&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can monitor and troubleshoot every layer of this multi-stage pipeline across SageMaker HyperPod, ECS, Lambda, and SQS. The infrastructure provides observability at every layer so you can isolate issues quickly.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="pipeline-monitoring"&gt;Pipeline monitoring&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Step Functions console is the primary dashboard. Each execution shows step-by-step progress with timing, input and output data, and retry history. Every step writes to its own Amazon CloudWatch Log Group.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;During normal operation, each Step Functions step completes within its expected time window: infrastructure setup in 2-3 minutes, reward worker deployment in 1-2 minutes, data validation in under 1 minute, and training submission in 3-5 minutes. SQS queues should show messages flowing steadily with no sustained backlog.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="failure-alerting"&gt;Failure alerting&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;An Amazon Simple Notification Service (Amazon SNS) topic, encrypted with AWS Key Management Service (AWS KMS), publishes a notification when a Step Functions execution fails. Subscribe to receive alerts by email, Slack, or PagerDuty:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;# Get the topic ARN from CDK outputs
TOPIC_ARN=$(aws cloudformation describe-stacks --stack-name NovaMultiTurnRlStack \
  --query "Stacks[0].Outputs[?OutputKey=='AlertTopicArn'].OutputValue" --output text)

# Subscribe and confirm via email
aws sns subscribe \
  --topic-arn $TOPIC_ARN \
  --protocol email \
  --notification-endpoint your-team@example.com&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="trigger-failures"&gt;Trigger failures&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If an S3 upload does not start the pipeline, check the dead-letter queue. Failed EventBridge-to-Lambda invocations are stored here with the original S3 event data and error details:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;DLQ_URL=$(aws cloudformation describe-stacks --stack-name NovaMultiTurnRlStack \
  --query "Stacks[0].Outputs[?OutputKey=='TriggerDlqUrl'].OutputValue" --output text)

aws sqs receive-message --queue-url $DLQ_URL --max-number-of-messages 5&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Common causes include malformed file names (the trigger expects a &lt;code&gt;.jsonl&lt;/code&gt; extension), IAM permission drift, and Lambda concurrency limits.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="training-stall-debugging"&gt;Training stall debugging&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If the pipeline starts but training stalls, the root cause is usually in the message-routing layer between the model and the reward environment. Check SQS queue health using the SDK built-in diagnostic:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;from rft_infra import check_all_queues

# Returns message counts (in-flight, available, delayed) for all FIFO queues
check_all_queues()&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A growing backlog in the request queue with an empty response queue indicates that reward workers are not processing. A growing backlog in the response queue indicates that the model is not consuming rewards. Either pattern tells you which component to investigate.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="hyperpod-health"&gt;HyperPod health&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Verify that all training and generation pods are running on the SageMaker HyperPod cluster:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl get pods -n kubeflow&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;All pods should show &lt;code&gt;Running&lt;/code&gt; or &lt;code&gt;Completed&lt;/code&gt; status. Pods stuck in &lt;code&gt;Pending&lt;/code&gt; indicate insufficient instance capacity in the Restricted Instance Group. Pods in &lt;code&gt;CrashLoopBackOff&lt;/code&gt; (a Kubernetes status indicating the container is repeatedly crashing and restarting) indicate container-level errors. Check the pod logs with &lt;code&gt;kubectl logs &amp;lt;pod-name&amp;gt; -n kubeflow&lt;/code&gt; for details.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up-and-manage-cost"&gt;Clean up and manage cost&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The cleanup script stops active resources in this order: Step Functions executions, ECS tasks, the SDK CloudFormation stack, and the CDK stack.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid charges, run the following command to destroy the entire stack:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;./cleanup.sh&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To destroy the infrastructure while keeping your training data in S3 (storage charges still apply):&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;./cleanup.sh --retain-data&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Important:&lt;/strong&gt; Destroy the stack when you are not actively training. SageMaker HyperPod instances and NAT Gateway idle costs accumulate quickly.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="cost-breakdown"&gt;Cost breakdown&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Resource&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Hourly Cost&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt;

  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt;

   &lt;td&gt;~$786/hr&lt;/td&gt;

  &lt;/tr&gt;

 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For current Amazon Nova Forge pricing, see the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ai/generative-ai/nova/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova pricing page&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="optimize-for-cost"&gt;Optimize for cost&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can reduce idle costs with two strategies:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Scale to zero:&lt;/strong&gt; Provision a single-instance cluster with automatic scaling policies that scale down to zero during periods of inactivity, eliminating idle compute costs.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Instance-type scheduling:&lt;/strong&gt; Use lower-cost instance types during setup and off-peak phases, then transition to higher-performance instances when training workloads begin.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You now have a production-ready, event-driven infrastructure for multi-turn reinforcement learning with Amazon Nova Forge on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod. Upload a dataset to Amazon S3, and the pipeline handles provisioning, training orchestration, and reward routing automatically. To adapt this to your use case, replace the Wordle environment with your own API-calling agent or enterprise workflow.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Next steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Clone the sample repository: &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/nova-multi-turn-rl-infra" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;aws-samples/nova-multi-turn-rl-infra&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Read the Amazon SageMaker HyperPod documentation.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Read about Amazon Nova capabilities and pricing.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Customize your pipeline with the AWS Step Functions developer guide.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Automatically redact PII in images with Amazon Nova</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/automatically-redact-pii-in-images-with-amazon-nova/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Caroline Des Rochers]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 06 Jul 2026 16:55:02 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">213c37353efd05356306bc675918c3841331fccd</guid>

					<description>In this post, we present a multi-step pipeline directed by Amazon Nova, which uses its contextual vision reasoning to coordinate complementary tools, including Meta’s open-source Segment Anything Model (SAM 3) deployed on Amazon SageMaker AI for pixel-level segmentation, and Amazon Textract for optical character recognition (OCR). This pipeline is designed to provide comprehensive and compliant PII redaction even for challenging edge cases such as fingerprints, ID cards, or license plates in arbitrary orientations.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Sharing data internally across teams, externally with partners, or using it for workloads such as machine learning (ML) model training is fundamental to modern business operations. However, when that data contains Personally Identifiable Information (PII), organizations face significant legal and compliance obligations under regulations such as the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) and the Payment Card Industry Data Security Standard (PCI DSS). If PII isn’t properly redacted before sharing or processing data, the result can be regulatory penalties, reputational damage, and erosion of customer trust.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;PII redaction in real-world image datasets is particularly challenging. Unlike structured text, PII in images can appear in unexpected places and forms: a partial face captured at the edge of a frame, a face reflected on the polished surface of a car, a partially visible street sign that, combined with other visual cues, becomes identifiable, or a document lying on a desk in a wide-angle photo that reveals names, addresses, or ID numbers. These edge cases routinely defeat single-purpose masking tools.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/nova/models/?trk=33dc490e-0fb2-4cb1-a521-3941c13b64c0&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova&lt;/a&gt; is a family of foundation models with advanced vision understanding capabilities, making it a strong candidate to serve as the intelligent coordinator for complex image analysis workflows. Nova interprets image content holistically, reasons about whether something constitutes PII in context, including the subtle and unusual cases described earlier, and directs the entire redaction pipeline from start to finish. By understanding the “what” of PII, Nova coordinates specialized tools to achieve pixel-level precision in redaction while preserving the overall value of the image.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we present a multi-step pipeline directed by Amazon Nova, which uses its contextual vision reasoning to coordinate complementary tools, including Meta’s open-source Segment Anything Model (SAM 3) deployed on &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/?trk=047fc009-5bd4-4337-800d-8b880665cece&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;trk=c3974822-373e-499b-9739-834a50722377&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=CjwKCAjwuO_QBhAWEiwAIkVhU_SEPtv0f-b7rSZQYrxPl_-56NFn16ZJoiNS46JpT4hYCiMKhkwu2xoCNY0QAvD_BwE:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!795877020716!e!!g!!sagemaker%20ai!23532472972!194311071804&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23532472972&amp;amp;gclid=CjwKCAjwuO_QBhAWEiwAIkVhU_SEPtv0f-b7rSZQYrxPl_-56NFn16ZJoiNS46JpT4hYCiMKhkwu2xoCNY0QAvD_BwE" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI&lt;/a&gt; for pixel-level segmentation, and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/textract/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/a&gt; for optical character recognition (OCR). This pipeline is designed to provide comprehensive and compliant PII redaction even for challenging edge cases such as fingerprints, ID cards, or license plates in arbitrary orientations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This solution uses the following key services to achieve comprehensive PII redaction:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="nova-2-lite"&gt;Nova 2 Lite&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Nova 2 Lite is a fast, cost-effective multimodal foundation model that processes text, images, videos, and documents. Available in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt;, the model offers strong price-performance and helps enterprises and developers build capable, reliable, and efficient applications. It serves as the central coordinator of this solution, coordinating the entire PII detection and redaction workflow, making decisions at every stage, and coordinating specialized services to achieve comprehensive results.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="segment-anything-model-sam-3"&gt;Segment Anything Model (SAM 3)&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;SAM 3 is an open-source segmentation model that detects, segments, and tracks objects in images from text and visual prompts. In this solution, SAM 3 acts as a precision instrument directed by Nova. When Nova identifies visual PII elements, it delegates the segmentation task to SAM 3 to produce exact boundaries for redaction.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20185-1.png" alt="SAM 3 segmentation masks overlaid on a photo of flowers" width="600" height="630"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;SAM 3 segmentation masks overlaid on the original image when prompted with the word ‘flower’ on model playground (https://ai.meta.com/research/sam3/)&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="amazon-textract"&gt;Amazon Textract&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Textract is an ML service that automatically extracts text, handwriting, layout elements, and data from formats such as PDFs, images, tables, and forms. In this pipeline, Amazon Textract serves as Nova’s OCR capability. When Nova determines that an image contains textual PII, it directs Amazon Textract to extract all text content along with their coordinates, which Nova then evaluates to determine what constitutes sensitive information, taking into account context of the entire image.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="pii-detection"&gt;PII detection&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Common PII items found in images include, but are not limited to:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Name.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Identification number, such as driver’s license number.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Address.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Telephone number.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Asset information, such as Media Access Control (MAC) address.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Property identification number, such as a vehicle identification number (VIN).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Personal characteristic, such as facial images.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Biometric data, such as fingerprints.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;These items can be divided into 2 modalities: textual (name, identification number, address, telephone number, asset information, and property identification number) and visual (personal characteristics and biometric data).&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Nova directs a workflow with access to two sub-processes, each identifying either the pixel coordinates of the textual or the visual PII items. Nova first performs an initial assessment of the image to determine what types of PII are present, then intelligently routes the image to the appropriate sub-processes. An AWS Lambda function subsequently obscures the contents at the coordinates identified under Nova’s direction.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This solution is ideal for one-off or batch image pre-processing scenarios where high redaction accuracy is required. Nova 2 Lite’s strong price-performance, low latency, and advanced multimodal reasoning make it well-suited to coordinate this workflow, delivering accurate PII redaction without requiring your organization to have deep machine learning expertise or to fine-tune purpose-built models.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To follow along with this solution, you should have:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS account.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Familiarity with Amazon Bedrock, Amazon SageMaker AI, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/s3/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Simple Storage Service&lt;/a&gt; (Amazon S3), &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/pm/lambda/?trk=787b43c8-35d2-4537-bf86-d42e7c121401&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps&amp;amp;ef_id=CjwKCAjwjtTNBhB0EiwAuswYhn24Uq2Bt6TTYg3nK2K9N_nKetvrkOs45tJOZ4-kVMJYfk1hZavFShoCVeoQAvD_BwE:G:s&amp;amp;s_kwcid=AL!4422!3!795794141653!e!!g!!amazon%20lambda!23527793966!192204324466&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=23527793966&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAADjHtp_DJwRcBBdbHLaptszTVpfxR&amp;amp;gclid=CjwKCAjwjtTNBhB0EiwAuswYhn24Uq2Bt6TTYg3nK2K9N_nKetvrkOs45tJOZ4-kVMJYfk1hZavFShoCVeoQAvD_BwE" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Lambda&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/step-functions/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Step Functions&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/eventbridge/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon EventBridge&lt;/a&gt;, and Amazon Textract.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Access to Nova 2 Lite via Amazon Bedrock in your AWS Region.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://github.com/facebookresearch/sam3" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Deploy SAM 3&lt;/a&gt; on SageMaker AI.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Basic knowledge of AWS Software Development Kits (SDK).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Basic understanding of computer vision concepts and image processing.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Familiarity with foundation models and prompt engineering techniques.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Please be aware that this solution will incur AWS charges including: S3 storage, Lambda invocations, Step Functions state transitions, SageMaker AI endpoint hosting, Amazon Bedrock API calls, and Amazon Textract API calls. Make sure you understand the pricing for these services in your AWS Region before you create the corresponding resources.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-architecture"&gt;Solution architecture&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This section describes an architectural guidance where Nova drives the image processing workflow and directs parallel textual and visual analysis to identify and redact sensitive information.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img loading="lazy" class="alignnone size-full wp-image-134418" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/25/ml-20185-arch.png" alt="Architecture diagram of the Amazon Nova-directed PII detection and redaction pipeline" width="889" height="1262"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="document-upload-and-workflow-trigger"&gt;1. Document upload and workflow trigger&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When you upload an image to the S3 &lt;code&gt;input/&lt;/code&gt; folder, an S3 event notification triggers an EventBridge rule, which initiates the AWS Step Functions workflow.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/20185-1.png" alt="Step 1: an image uploaded to the Amazon S3 originals folder triggers the Step Functions workflow" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="file-validation-and-initial-pii-screening"&gt;2. File validation and initial PII screening&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The workflow first validates that your uploaded file type is compatible with Amazon Bedrock supported image formats. After validation, Nova 2 Lite performs its initial assessment of the image, acting as the first line of intelligence to determine whether PII is present. If Nova determines that no PII exists, the workflow exits early and the image is immediately moved to the S3 noPII/ folder, ready for downstream use without further processing. Most business images contain no PII. This early-exit decision by Nova routes away a large number of images at the first and least expensive step. It significantly reduces overall pipeline cost by avoiding unnecessary invocations of downstream services such as Amazon Textract and SAM 3 on SageMaker AI.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/20185-2.png" alt="Step 2: Nova 2 Lite validates the file and screens for PII before further processing" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="parallel-pii-detection-visual-process"&gt;3. Parallel PII detection: Visual process&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When Nova 2 Lite identifies potential PII, it classifies the detected PII by type, textual, visual, or both, and makes the routing decision for downstream processing. Based on Nova’s classification, the Step Functions workflow selectively invokes one or both specialized processes: the textual process if Nova detected textual PII, the visual process if Nova detected visual PII, or both processes in parallel if Nova identified both types.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The visual process focuses on detecting visual PII elements such as faces and license plates within your image. Under Nova’s direction, SAM 3, deployed on SageMaker AI, is prompted to locate visual PII elements and produce pixel-by-pixel segmentation masks. A segmentation mask is a pixel-level outline that traces the exact shape of an object in an image. It labels each pixel as either belonging to that object or not, similar to a precise digital stencil. Unlike a simple bounding box (a rough rectangle around an object), a segmentation mask follows the true contours of the detected item, allowing redaction of only the sensitive pixels without obscuring surrounding content. This precision approach, guided by Nova’s initial PII classification, helps maintain the image’s value while removing only the sensitive regions. For example, when training ML models on large image datasets, this method helps prevent PII from being passed to the model while preserving your dataset size and non-sensitive visual information.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/20185-3.png" alt="Step 3: SAM 3 on SageMaker AI generates segmentation masks for visual PII" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="parallel-pii-detection-textual-process"&gt;4. Parallel PII detection: Textual process&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The textual process uses Nova’s multimodal reasoning to analyze text embedded within your image with the help of Amazon Textract. First, Amazon Textract identifies pixel-level location of text in images and extracts its content. It provides both axis-aligned bounding box and polygon coordinates for each detected text element, and you can choose which coordinate type best suits your redaction needs. Nova 2 Lite then evaluates each extracted text element, alongside the raw image, to determine which ones contain PII such as names, addresses, or identification numbers. This is where Nova’s contextual intelligence is critical: some text elements, when considered in isolation, might not appear to be PII, but Nova can reason about the surrounding visual context in the image to recognize their sensitive nature. For example, a street name and a street number broken into 2 locations do not constitute PII separately by themselves, but because they both appear in the same image, the full address is visible and should be obscured as PII. As a multimodal model, Nova can process text and image simultaneously to make these nuanced PII determinations. Finally, Amazon Textract returns the coordinates of the text that Nova identified as PII for use in the next step.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="pii-redaction-and-final-verification"&gt;5. PII redaction and final verification&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After the sub-process(es) complete their analysis under Nova’s direction, their coordinate outputs are passed to the next stage for redaction. Recall that the output from the visual process is segmentation mask coordinates from SAM 3, while the output from the textual process is bounding box or polygon coordinates from Amazon Textract.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A Lambda function merges coordinates returned from the process(es) into a unified set of PII locations across your image. The &lt;code&gt;RedactPII&lt;/code&gt; Lambda function then obscures the identified regions in the original image using the &lt;code&gt;Pillow&lt;/code&gt; (&lt;code&gt;PIL&lt;/code&gt;) library in Python and stores the redacted image in the S3 redacted/ folder.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;As a final quality assurance step, Nova 2 Lite performs a comprehensive review of the redacted image to decide if all detected PII has been removed. This final verification uses Nova’s holistic vision understanding to catch any residual sensitive content. If Nova determines that the image is clean, it is moved to the S3 noPII/ folder for downstream use. If Nova still detects PII, the image is moved to a quarantine folder for manual review, designed to help prevent PII from slipping through.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img loading="lazy" class="alignnone wp-image-134419 size-full" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/25/ml-20185-p5.png" alt="Step 5: a Lambda function redacts identified regions and Nova verifies the result" width="1080" height="586"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="test-the-solution"&gt;6. Test the solution&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To verify that the solution is working correctly, follow these steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="a"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Use a sample test image containing PII. You can use an AI-generated image.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Upload the image to the S3 originals/ bucket using the AWS Command Line Interface (AWS CLI) or the Amazon S3 console.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Monitor the Step Functions execution on the AWS Step Functions console to track the workflow progress.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Verify the redacted image appears in the S3 redacted/ or humanreview/ folder.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Download the output image from the S3 redacted/ or humanreview/ folder.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Inspect the image to check if PII has been successfully removed, or identify PII elements (for example, niche elements beyond those cited in the “PII detection” section) that require human review.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20185-7.jpeg" alt="AI-generated sample photo with a visible face and street address before redaction" width="600" height="644"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;AI-generated image with textual PII (address) and visual PII (face)&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20185-8.jpeg" alt="The same sample photo after the pipeline redacts the face and address" width="600" height="636"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;PII elements redacted in the same image by pipeline using bounding box coordinates from Amazon Textract and segmentation mask coordinates from SAM 3&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Complete the following steps to clean up your resources when you no longer need this solution. Before proceeding with cleanup, make sure you have backed up any data you need to retain from the S3 buckets. The following steps will permanently delete all resources and data.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Back up any necessary data from the S3 buckets.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the S3 buckets (&lt;code&gt;originals/&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;noPII/&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;redacted/&lt;/code&gt;, and &lt;code&gt;humanreview/&lt;/code&gt;)&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the SageMaker AI endpoint hosting the SAM 3 model.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the Step Functions state machine.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the Lambda functions (PII detection, redaction, and final verification).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the EventBridge rule configured for S3 event notifications.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we demonstrated how Amazon Nova 2 Lite serves as the intelligent coordinator of a serverless, automated pipeline for detecting and redacting PII from images without requiring custom model training or fine-tuning. Nova’s multimodal reasoning drives every decision in the workflow: from initial PII detection and classification, through intelligent routing to specialized tools like Amazon Textract and SAM 3 on Amazon SageMaker AI, to final quality verification of the redacted output. With Nova 2 Lite’s strong price-performance and advanced vision capabilities, organizations can deploy PII redaction at scale while maintaining full compliance with data protection regulations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This post provides architectural guidance that you can adapt to your specific compliance requirements and deploy in your own AWS account. To get started, explore Amazon Nova models in the &lt;a href="https://console.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Amazon Bedrock console&lt;/a&gt;, and refer to the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/nova/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Amazon Nova documentation&lt;/a&gt;, the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/textract/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Amazon Textract documentation&lt;/a&gt;, and the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI documentation&lt;/a&gt; to deploy the complementary services used in this pipeline.&lt;/p&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Streaming benchmark and recommendation results to MLflow with Amazon SageMaker AI</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/streaming-benchmark-and-recommendation-results-to-mlflow-with-amazon-sagemaker-ai/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Mona Mona]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 06 Jul 2026 16:53:38 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Announcements]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">0a4fbb45ba71b55244cdd6eb52c29ff670f0d44a</guid>

					<description>In this post, you learn how to use the new MLflow integration with Amazon SageMaker AI optimized inference recommendation jobs and Amazon SageMaker AI benchmark jobs to automatically stream experiment data into a unified tracking interface. This integration streams metrics, parameters, and charts into your serverless Amazon SageMaker MLflow App in real time and you get a unified experiment tracking experience.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Teams benchmarking generative AI models often evaluate dozens of GPU instance types, serving containers, parallelism strategies, and optimization techniques such as speculative decoding before deploying to production. Practitioners can spend weeks navigating configuration decisions and manually piecing together what they tried, what worked, and why. That complexity is exactly why we introduced &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/amazon-sagemaker-ai-now-supports-optimized-generative-ai-inference-recommendations/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;optimized generative AI inference recommendations for Amazon SageMaker AI&lt;/a&gt;: to help teams move from manual trial-and-error to guided, data-driven optimization and benchmarking.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Today, we are adding MLflow integration so teams can stream AI benchmark and recommendation results into a single place to track every experiment. This integration reduces data silos, accelerates iteration cycles, and brings full reproducibility to your inference optimization workflows.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you learn how to use the new MLflow integration with &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/generative-ai-inference-recommendations.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI optimized inference recommendation jobs&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/APIReference/API_CreateAIBenchmarkJob.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI benchmark jobs&lt;/a&gt; to automatically stream experiment data into a unified tracking interface.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This integration streams metrics, parameters, and charts into your serverless &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/mlflow-app-setup.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker MLflow App&lt;/a&gt; in real time and you get a unified experiment tracking experience.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With this release, when you submit an optimized inference recommendation job or a benchmarking job, Amazon SageMaker AI automatically streams results into a SageMaker MLflow app of your choice. Submit multiple jobs to the same MLflow experiment, and you can select them in the MLflow experiment view to compare side by side, with no manual data wrangling required.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following diagram shows how you can set up MLflow with your benchmarking and recommendation jobs:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21187-1.jpeg" alt="Architecture diagram showing benchmark and recommendation jobs streaming results into a SageMaker MLflow app" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You follow these steps to set up MLflow with your existing recommendations and benchmarking jobs:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Create an MLflow App&lt;/strong&gt;: In your AWS account, open Amazon SageMaker Studio. Then go to &lt;strong&gt;MLflow&lt;/strong&gt; and choose &lt;strong&gt;Create MLflow App&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grant permissions:&lt;/strong&gt; Add &lt;code&gt;sagemaker-mlflow:*&lt;/code&gt; on the MLflow App ARN to your job’s execution role.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Pass &lt;code&gt;MlflowConfig&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/strong&gt; when creating your benchmark or recommendation job.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="benefits-of-this-implementation"&gt;Benefits of this implementation&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Some of the benefits of this implementation are:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Eliminate manual data consolidation:&lt;/strong&gt; With native MLflow integration, benchmark and recommendation results from multiple jobs are consolidated under the same experiment name automatically. This removes the need to manually collect metrics, logs, and configurations across separate runs. You can then compare runs side by side to understand how instance type, model configuration, batch size, or speculative decoding affects performance. For example, you can compare &lt;code&gt;qwen2-0.5b&lt;/code&gt; on &lt;code&gt;ml.g4dn.12xlarge&lt;/code&gt; versus &lt;code&gt;ml.p4d.24xlarge&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Monitor long-running jobs in real time:&lt;/strong&gt; Benchmark and recommendation jobs can take hours. Rather than waiting for completion without visibility, you can now watch metrics stream live into the MLflow UI. Latency and throughput metrics update as each configuration is tested, giving you an observable process from start to finish. We publish live metrics in the metrics section, and they update over time instead of being published only at the end of the job. You can use these metrics to check whether throughput is as expected in the recommendation job and stop it early if it is not.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Maintain a complete audit trail:&lt;/strong&gt; Experiment runs capture the full context: job parameters, timestamps, metrics at checkpoints, and emitted artifacts. This audit trail stays queryable and reproducible for months. You can trace which configuration changes drove improvements, identify performance plateaus, and revisit past decisions with full context.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Better collaboration and governance:&lt;/strong&gt; A shared MLflow experiment becomes the single source of truth for an optimization effort. Team members can review what’s been tried, what worked, and what didn’t, which reduces duplicated effort and supports informed handoffs between shifts or team members.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="technical-walkthrough-track-sagemaker-ai-benchmarking-and-recommendations-in-mlflow"&gt;Technical walkthrough: Track SageMaker AI benchmarking and recommendations in MLflow&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this example, a customer has deployed &lt;code&gt;Qwen/Qwen2-0.5B-Instruct&lt;/code&gt; to an Amazon SageMaker AI real-time endpoint using an OpenAI-compatible serving stack on ml.g6.12xlarge. The team has two questions to answer: how the existing Qwen endpoint performs for a lightweight chatbot workload, and what configuration to use for a larger workload with longer prompts and responses.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To make the evaluation reproducible, both the benchmark job and recommendation job write metrics, parameters, and charts to the same SageMaker MLflow experiment. The benchmark job evaluates an existing SageMaker endpoint. The recommendation job evaluates deployment options for the Qwen model artifact stored in Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3) and returns ranked configurations. We will use a walkthrough notebook to show you how you can set up MLflow with both recommendations using &lt;code&gt;Qwen/Qwen2-0.5B-Instruct&lt;/code&gt; on &lt;code&gt;ml.g6.12xlarge&lt;/code&gt; instance for latency optimization&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The end-to-end walkthrough typically takes &lt;strong&gt;45–120 minutes&lt;/strong&gt;, depending on endpoint readiness, model size, workload configuration, instance availability, and recommendation job search space.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sagemaker-genai-hosting-examples/blob/main/03-features/gen-ai-inference-recommendations/gen-ai-inference-recommendations-and-benchmarking-mlflow.ipynb" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;notebook&lt;/a&gt; walks you through how you can set up your MLflow app for benchmark and recommendations jobs experiment tracking. You need to complete the following steps to get started with running this notebook.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Set up &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/studio/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker Studio&lt;/a&gt; for your development environment on Amazon SageMaker AI.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create a &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/mlflow-app-setup.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SageMaker MLflow App&lt;/a&gt; from SageMaker Unified Studio (SageMaker Unified Studio → MLflow → Create MLflow App).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A &lt;strong&gt;SageMaker endpoint&lt;/strong&gt; running an OpenAI-compatible model (already InService) for the benchmark job. You can deploy the Qwen 2 0.5B model from the Amazon SageMaker JumpStart Studio UI to get started as shown in the following diagram:&lt;br&gt; &lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21187-2.jpeg" alt="Amazon SageMaker JumpStart Studio UI for deploying the Qwen 2 0.5B model" width="800"&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A model artifact in Amazon S3 for the recommendation job.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) execution role with &lt;code&gt;AmazonSageMakerFullAccess&lt;/code&gt; plus inline policies for MLflow and endpoint invoke access. See the accompanying notebook for IAM role creation steps.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="key-considerations"&gt;Key considerations&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;This integration supports SageMaker MLflow Apps and does not stream results to self-hosted MLflow tracking servers.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Set &lt;code&gt;tooling.version&lt;/code&gt; to 0.8.0 or later to use MLflow nested run support.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Recommendation jobs provision their own endpoints internally, so do not pass &lt;code&gt;ComputeSpec.InstanceTypes&lt;/code&gt; unless you have a specific reason to constrain the search space. The execution role must include the required SageMaker, MLflow, Amazon S3, and endpoint invocation permissions. The S3 output bucket must be in the same Region as the job.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For the complete end-to-end notebook, including additional examples with &lt;code&gt;AIRecommendationJob&lt;/code&gt;, see the step-by-step tutorial.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-1-set-up-your-environment"&gt;Step 1: Set up your environment&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Start by setting your environment variables and initializing the SageMaker client.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import json
import time
import uuid

import boto3
import botocore.config

# Update these values for your environment
REGION = "us-west-2"
ACCOUNT_ID = "&amp;lt;your-account-id&amp;gt;"
MLFLOW_APP_ARN = "arn:aws:sagemaker:us-west-2:&amp;lt;account-id&amp;gt;:mlflow-app/&amp;lt;app-id&amp;gt;"
ENDPOINT_NAME = "my-model-endpoint"

sm = boto3.client(
    "sagemaker",
    region_name=REGION,
    config=botocore.config.Config(
        retries={"mode": "standard", "max_attempts": 15}
    ),
)

# The S3 output bucket must be in the same Region as the job
S3_OUTPUT_BUCKET = f"mlflow-sagemaker-{REGION}-{ACCOUNT_ID}"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-2-resolve-the-execution-role"&gt;Step 2: Resolve the execution role&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI uses the execution role to run the benchmark and recommendation jobs. It must have permissions to access SageMaker, invoke endpoints, write job output to Amazon S3, and publish tracking information to the SageMaker MLflow App. Follow the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sagemaker-genai-hosting-examples/blob/main/03-features/gen-ai-inference-recommendations/gen-ai-inference-recommendations-and-benchmarking-mlflow.ipynb" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;notebook&lt;/a&gt; to create the IAM roles.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The role should include the following permissions:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table style="height: 219px" border="1px" width="994" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Permission area&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Required access&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;SageMaker job execution&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;AmazonSageMakerFullAccess&lt;/code&gt;, or a scoped equivalent&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;MLflow integration&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;sagemaker-mlflow:*&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;sagemaker:CallMlflowAppApi&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;sagemaker:DescribeMlflowApp&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Endpoint invocation&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;sagemaker:InvokeEndpoint&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;sagemaker:InvokeEndpointWithResponseStream&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;S3 output&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Read/write access to the configured output bucket&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;ROLE_NAME = "tutorial-exec-role"

iam = boto3.client("iam")
role = iam.get_role(RoleName=ROLE_NAME)["Role"]
ROLE_ARN = role["Arn"]

print(f"Role ARN: {ROLE_ARN}")&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Configure the execution role trust policy to allow SageMaker to assume the role. In some environments, you may also need to include the account root principal depending on how the role is created and passed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-3-verify-the-mlflow-app-and-define-experiment-names"&gt;Step 3: Verify the MLflow App and define experiment names&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Next, verify that the SageMaker MLflow App is available. Then define a shared experiment name and unique run names for the benchmark and recommendation jobs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Using the same &lt;code&gt;MlflowExperimentName&lt;/code&gt; for both jobs makes it easier to compare results in one place.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;# Verify that the MLflow App is available
app = sm.describe_mlflow_app(Arn=MLFLOW_APP_ARN)
MLFLOW_APP_URL = app["Url"]

assert app["Status"] in ("Created", "Updated")

print(f"MLflow URL: {MLFLOW_APP_URL}")

# Define a shared experiment and unique run names
MLFLOW_EXPERIMENT = "gemma-experiments"
SESSION_UID = uuid.uuid4().hex[:6]

BENCH_RUN_NAME = f"benchmark-{SESSION_UID}"
REC_RUN_NAME = f"recommendation-{SESSION_UID}"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-4-submit-the-benchmark-job"&gt;Step 4: Submit the benchmark job&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A SageMaker AI benchmark job evaluates an existing endpoint using a workload specification. In this example, the workload uses NVIDIA AIPerf with an OpenAI-compatible API schema.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The key field is &lt;code&gt;MlflowConfig&lt;/code&gt; in &lt;code&gt;OutputConfig&lt;/code&gt;. This tells SageMaker to stream benchmark metrics, parameters, and charts directly into your SageMaker MLflow App.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;# Tokenizer MUST match the deployed model.
# tooling.version=0.8.0 is the minimum for MLflow nested run support.
TOKENIZER = "Qwen/Qwen2-0.5B-Instruct"
bench_workload = {
    "benchmark": {"type": "aiperf"},
    "parameters": {
        "model": TOKENIZER,
        "concurrency": 1,
        "request_count": 3,
        "streaming": True,
        "prompt_input_tokens_mean": 32,
        "prompt_input_tokens_stddev": 8,
        "output_tokens_mean": 16,
        "output_tokens_stddev": 4,
        "random_seed": 42,
    },
    "tooling": {
        "api_standard": "openai",
        "version": "0.8.0",
    },
}

BENCH_CONFIG = f"mlflow-bench-cfg-{SESSION_UID}"
BENCH_JOB = f"mlflow-bench-job-{SESSION_UID}"

sm.create_ai_workload_config(
    AIWorkloadConfigName=BENCH_CONFIG,
    AIWorkloadConfigs={
        "WorkloadSpec": {
            "Inline": json.dumps(bench_workload)
        }
    },
)

sm.create_ai_benchmark_job(
    AIBenchmarkJobName=BENCH_JOB,
    AIWorkloadConfigIdentifier=BENCH_CONFIG,
    RoleArn=ROLE_ARN,
    BenchmarkTarget={
        "Endpoint": {
            "Identifier": ENDPOINT_NAME
        }
    },
    OutputConfig={
        "S3OutputLocation": f"s3://{S3_OUTPUT_BUCKET}/mlflow-tutorial/{BENCH_JOB}/",
        "MlflowConfig": {
            "MlflowResourceArn": MLFLOW_APP_ARN,
            "MlflowExperimentName": MLFLOW_EXPERIMENT,
            "MlflowRunName": BENCH_RUN_NAME,
        },
    },
)

print(f"Benchmark job submitted: {BENCH_JOB}")&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-5-submit-the-recommendation-job"&gt;Step 5: Submit the recommendation job&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;SageMaker AI recommendation jobs evaluate deployment configurations and recommend options based on your workload and performance target. Unlike benchmark jobs, recommendation jobs provision their own endpoints internally during the evaluation process.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following example uses the same MLflow experiment name as the benchmark job, so both runs appear together in the SageMaker MLflow App.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;rec_workload = {
    "benchmark": {"type": "aiperf"},
    "parameters": {
        "prompt_input_tokens_mean": 1600,
        "prompt_input_tokens_stddev": 200,
        "output_tokens_mean": 600,
        "output_tokens_stddev": 100,
        "request_count": 100,
    },
    "tooling": {
        "api_standard": "openai",
        "version": "0.8.0",
    },
}

REC_CONFIG = f"mlflow-rec-cfg-{SESSION_UID}"
REC_JOB = f"mlflow-rec-job-{SESSION_UID}"

sm.create_ai_workload_config(
    AIWorkloadConfigName=REC_CONFIG,
    AIWorkloadConfigs={
        "WorkloadSpec": {
            "Inline": json.dumps(rec_workload)
        }
    },
)

sm.create_ai_recommendation_job(
    AIRecommendationJobName=REC_JOB,
    ModelSource={
        "S3": {
            "S3Uri": MODEL_S3_URI
        }
    },
    OutputConfig={
        "S3OutputLocation": f"s3://{S3_OUTPUT_BUCKET}/mlflow-tutorial/{REC_JOB}/",
        "MlflowConfig": {
            "MlflowResourceArn": MLFLOW_APP_ARN,
            "MlflowExperimentName": MLFLOW_EXPERIMENT,
            "MlflowRunName": REC_RUN_NAME,
        },
    },
    RoleArn=ROLE_ARN,
    AIWorkloadConfigIdentifier=REC_CONFIG,
    PerformanceTarget={
        "Constraints": [
            {
                "Metric": "ttft-ms"
            }
        ]
    },
    OptimizeModel=False,
)

print(f"Recommendation job submitted: {REC_JOB}")&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-6-compare-results-in-mlflow"&gt;Step 6: Compare results in MLflow&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After the jobs complete, open the SageMaker MLflow App and navigate to the experiment you configured. The following code gives you the URL to your MLflow app in the notebook.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;print(f"Open: {MLFLOW_APP_URL}")&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In the experiment view, the parent runs are empty placeholders that exist to show relationships between child runs. You need to expand each parent run to view the nested metrics.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;pre class="text"&gt;&lt;code&gt;benchmark-&amp;lt;uid&amp;gt;        -&amp;gt; Single run with AIPerf benchmark metrics
recommendation-&amp;lt;uid&amp;gt;   -&amp;gt; Nested run tree with ranked recommendation configurations&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The child runs contain the model metrics. To review recommendation job metrics, go to the MLflow Runs page, expand the recommendation job parent run, and then expand the nested child runs until the concurrency rows are visible. The benchmark metrics appear in the Metrics columns for each concurrency run as shown in the following diagram:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21187-3.png" alt="MLflow Runs page showing benchmark metrics in the Metrics columns for each concurrency run" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To inspect recommendations artifacts, open the &lt;strong&gt;Concurrency=4&lt;/strong&gt; run and choose the &lt;strong&gt;Artifacts&lt;/strong&gt; tab. In the artifact file tree, expand &lt;strong&gt;exports&lt;/strong&gt; and select &lt;strong&gt;outputs.json&lt;/strong&gt;. The preview panel shows the artifact file path and JSON output, including request-level fields such as session ID, request timing, output token count, request latency, and response text as shown in the following diagram:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21187-4.png" alt="MLflow Artifacts tab showing the outputs.json file with request-level fields for the Concurrency=4 run" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;On the Runs page, select the benchmark runs you want to compare, such as the Concurrency=32, Concurrency=16, Concurrency=8, and Concurrency=4 rows. Choose Compare to view metric differences across duration, throughput, output token count, and request latency as shown in the following diagram:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21187-5.png" alt="MLflow comparison view of benchmark metrics across the Concurrency=32, 16, 8, and 4 runs" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In the Metrics section, use the Show diff only toggle to view only changed metrics. The table compares benchmark metrics such as benchmark duration and end-to-end output token throughput across the selected benchmark or recommendation job results as shown in the following diagram:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21187-6.png" alt="MLflow Metrics section with the Show diff only toggle comparing benchmark duration and output token throughput" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up-resources"&gt;Clean up resources&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid ongoing charges, delete the resources you created during this walkthrough. Remove the SageMaker MLflow App from SageMaker Unified Studio and delete the benchmark and recommendation jobs. Terminate any SageMaker endpoints you deployed for testing and remove the S3 output objects if they are no longer needed. If you created a dedicated IAM role for this tutorial, delete it along with its inline policies.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The MLflow integration for SageMaker AI &lt;code&gt;BenchmarkJob&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;AIRecommendationJob&lt;/code&gt; makes experiment results easier to track and understand. By streaming results directly into a familiar tracking interface, this integration removes the manual overhead that slows you down and provides real-time monitoring that saves compute costs. It also provides the reproducibility that production machine learning (ML) workflows demand.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Start comparing experiments in seconds. Add &lt;code&gt;MlflowConfig&lt;/code&gt; to your next benchmark job, open the generated runs in MLflow, and compare metrics such as benchmark duration, throughput, output token count, and request latency across configurations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;To learn more about Amazon SageMaker AI optimization capabilities, visit the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SageMaker documentation&lt;/a&gt; and the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sagemaker-genai-hosting-examples/blob/main/03-features/gen-ai-inference-recommendations/gen-ai-inference-recommendations-and-benchmarking-mlflow.ipynb" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;GitHub&lt;/a&gt; notebook to get started.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;

   &lt;p&gt;&lt;img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-full" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21187-10.jpg" alt="Shen Teng" width="100" height="100"&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
